# big-code-rust-refac-001: Rename SubtypePredicate to SubtypeRelation in the Rust Compiler

## Task

Rename the `SubtypePredicate` struct to `SubtypeRelation` and its fields `a`/`b` to `sub_ty`/`super_ty` throughout the Rust compiler. The current `SubtypePredicate<I: Interner>` struct in `compiler/rustc_type_ir/src/predicate.rs` uses opaque field names `a` and `b` for what are semantically the subtype and supertype in a subtype relation. This refactoring improves clarity across 9 compiler crates.

The refactoring includes:
1. Rename the struct `SubtypePredicate` to `SubtypeRelation` in both `rustc_type_ir` and `rustc_public`
2. Rename fields `a` to `sub_ty` and `b` to `super_ty`
3. Update all type aliases (`SubtypePredicate<'tcx>`, `PolySubtypePredicate<'tcx>`) in `rustc_middle`
4. Update all re-exports, imports, and `IrPrint` bounds
5. Update all construction sites (struct literal expressions) in `rustc_infer`, `rustc_type_ir`, `rustc_next_trait_solver`
6. Update all destructure/pattern-match sites across `rustc_hir_typeck`, `rustc_trait_selection`, `rustc_type_ir`, `rustc_infer`
7. Update the `PredicateKind::Subtype` variant's data type annotation

## Context

- **Repository**: rust-lang/rust (Rust, ~2.2M LOC)
- **Category**: Cross-File Refactoring
- **Difficulty**: hard
- **Subsystem Focus**: compiler/rustc_type_ir, rustc_middle, rustc_infer, rustc_trait_selection, rustc_hir_typeck, rustc_next_trait_solver, rustc_public

## Requirements

1. Identify ALL files that need modification for this refactoring
2. Document the complete dependency chain showing why each file is affected (respect the crate DAG)
3. Implement the changes (or describe them precisely if the scope is too large)
4. Verify that no references to the old names remain

   102→            | FulfillmentErrorCode::Subtype(_, _)

   106→            }
   107→        }
   108→    }
   109→}

Self::new_from_args(interner, def_id, args, term)\n   433\u2192    }\n   434\u2192\n   435\u2192    /// Extracts the underlying existential trait reference from this projection.\n   436\u2192    /// 477\u2192\n   478\u2192    pub fn item_def_id(&self) -> I::DefId {\n   479\u2192        self.skip_binder().def_id\n   480\u2192    }\n   481\u2192}\n   482\u2192\n   483\u2192#[derive(Clone, Copy, PartialEq, Eq, PartialOrd, Ord, Hash, Debug)]\n   484\u2192#[cfg_attr(feature = \"nightly\", derive(Encodable, Decodable, HashStable_NoContext))]\n   485\u2192pub enum AliasTermKind {\n   486\u2192    /// A projection `<Type as Trait>::AssocType`.\n   487\u2192    /// Can get normalized away if monomorphic enough.\n   488\u2192    ProjectionTy,\n   489\u2192    /// An associated type in an inherent `impl`\n   490\u2192    InherentTy,\n   491\u2192    /// An opaque type (usually from `impl Trait` in type aliases or function return types)\n   492\u2192    /// Can only be normalized away in PostAnalysis mode or its defining scope.\n   493\u2192    OpaqueTy,\n   494\u2192    /// A free type alias that actually checks its trait bounds.\n   495\u2192    /// Currently only used if the type alias references opaque types.\n   496\u2192    /// Can always be normalized away.\n   497\u2192    FreeTy,\n   498\u2192\n   499\u2192    /// An unevaluated anonymous constants.\n   500\u2192    UnevaluatedConst,\n   501\u2192    /// An unevaluated const coming from an associated const.\n   502\u2192    ProjectionConst,\n   503\u2192    /// A top level const item not part of a trait or 510\u2192    pub fn descr(self) -> &'static str {\n   511\u2192        match self {\n   512\u2192            AliasTermKind::ProjectionTy => \"associated type\",\n   513\u2192            AliasTermKind::ProjectionConst => \"associated const\",\n   514\u2192            AliasTermKind::InherentTy => \"inherent associated type\",\n   515\u2192            AliasTermKind::InherentConst => AliasTermKind::FreeConst => \"unevaluated constant\",\n   519\u2192            AliasTermKind::UnevaluatedConst => \"unevaluated constant\",\n   520\u2192        }\n   521\u2192    }\n   522\u2192\n   523\u2192    pub fn is_type(self) -> bool {\n   524\u2192        match self {\n   525\u2192            AliasTermKind::ProjectionTy\n   526\u2192            | AliasTermKind::InherentTy\n   527\u2192            | AliasTermKind::OpaqueTy\n   528\u2192            | AliasTermKind::FreeTy => true,\n   529\u2192\n   530\u2192            AliasTermKind::UnevaluatedConst\n   531\u2192            | 536\u2192}\n   537\u2192\n   538\u2192impl From<ty::AliasTyKind> for AliasTermKind {\n   539\u2192    fn from(value: ty::AliasTyKind) -> Self {\n   540\u2192        match value {\n   541\u2192 550\u2192///\n   551\u2192/// * For a projection, this would be `<Ty as Trait<...>>::N<...>`.\n   552\u2192/// * For an inherent projection, this would be `Ty::N<...>`.\n   553\u2192/// * For an Lift_Generic)]\n   556\u2192#[cfg_attr(\n   557\u2192    feature = \"nightly\",\n   558\u2192    derive(Decodable_NoContext, Encodable_NoContext, HashStable_NoContext)\n   559\u2192)]\n   560\u2192pub the trait and the\n   564\u2192    /// GAT parameters, if there are any.\n   565\u2192    ///\n   566\u2192    /// For an inherent projection, they consist of the self type and the GAT parameters,\n   567\u2192    /// if there are any.\n   568\u2192    ///\n   569\u2192    /// For RPIT the generic parameters are for the generics of the function,\n   570\u2192    /// while for TAIT it is used for 747\u2192\n   748\u2192impl<I: Interner> From<ty::UnevaluatedConst<I>> for AliasTerm<I> {\n   749\u2192    fn from(ct: ty::UnevaluatedConst<I>) -> Self {\n   750\u2192        AliasTerm { args: ct.args, def_id: ct.def.into(), _use_alias_term_new_instead: () }\n   751\u2192    }\n   752\u2192}\n   753\u2192\n   754\u2192/// This kind of predicate has no *direct* correspondent in the\n   755\u2192/// syntax, but it roughly corresponds to the syntactic forms:\n   756\u2192///\n   757\u2192/// 1. `T: TraitRef<..., Item = Type>`\n   758\u2192/// 2. `<T as TraitRef<...>>::Item == Type` (NYI)\n   759\u2192///\n   760\u2192/// In particular, form #1 is \"desugared\" to the combination of a\n   761\u2192/// normal trait predicate (`T: TraitRef<...>`) and one of these\n   762\u2192/// predicates. Form #2 is a broader form in that it also permits\n   763\u2192/// equality between arbitrary types. Processing an instance of\n   764\u2192/// Form #2 eventually yields one of these `ProjectionPredicate`\n   765\u2192/// instances to normalize the LHS.\n   766\u2192#[derive_where(Clone, Copy, Hash, PartialEq; I: Interner)]\n   767\u2192#[derive(TypeVisitable_Generic, TypeFoldable_Generic, Lift_Generic)]\n   768\u2192#[cfg_attr(\n   769\u2192    feature = \"nightly\",\n   770\u2192    derive(Decodable_NoContext, Encodable_NoContext, HashStable_NoContext)\n   771\u2192)]\n   772\u2192pub struct ProjectionPredicate<I: Interner> {\n   773\u2192    pub projection_term: AliasTerm<I>,\n   774\u2192    pub term: I::Term,\n   775\u2192}\n   776\u2192\n   777\u2192impl<I: Interner> Eq for ProjectionPredicate<I> {}\n   778\u2192\n   779\u2192impl<I: Interner> ProjectionPredicate<I> {\n   780\u2192    pub fn self_ty(self) -> I::Ty {\n   781\u2192        self.projection_term.self_ty()\n   782\u2192    }\n   783\u2192\n   784\u2192    pub fn with_replaced_self_ty(self, interner: I, self_ty: I::Ty) -> ProjectionPredicate<I> {\n   785\u2192        Self {\n   786\u2192            projection_term: self.projection_term.with_replaced_self_ty(interner, self_ty),\n   787\u2192            ..self\n   788\u2192        }\n   789\u2192    }\n   790\u2192\n   791\u2192    pub fn trait_def_id(self, interner: I) -> I::TraitId {\n   792\u2192        self.projection_term.trait_def_id(interner)\n   793\u2192    }\n   794\u2192\n   795\u2192    pub fn def_id(self) -> I::DefId {\n   796\u2192        self.projection_term.def_id\n   797\u2192    }\n   798\u2192}\n   799\u2192\n   800\u2192impl<I: Interner> ty::Binder<I, ProjectionPredicate<I>> {\n   801\u2192    /// Returns the `DefId` of the trait of the associated item being projected.\n 807\u2192    pub fn term(&self) -> ty::Binder<I, I::Term> {\n   808\u2192        self.map_bound(|predicate| predicate.term)\n   809\u2192    }\n   810\u2192\n   811\u2192    /// The `DefId` of the (ExistentialPredicate::AutoTrait(ref a), ExistentialPredicate::AutoTrait(ref b)) => {\n   255\u2192                tcx.def_path_hash(*a).cmp(&tcx.def_path_hash(*b))\n   256\u2192            }\n   257\u2192            (ExistentialPredicate::Trait(_), _) => Ordering::Less,\n   258\u2192            (ExistentialPredicate::Projection(_), ExistentialPredicate::Trait(_)) => {\n   259\u2192                Ordering::Greater\n   260\u2192            }\n   261\u2192            (ExistentialPredicate::Projection(_), _) => Ordering::Less,\n   262\u2192            (ExistentialPredicate::AutoTrait(_), _) => Ordering::Greater,\n   263\u2192        }\n   264\u2192    }\n   265\u2192}\n   266\u2192\n   267\u2192pub type PolyExistentialPredicate<'tcx> = ty::Binder<'tcx, ExistentialPredicate<'tcx>>;\n   268\u2192\n   269\u2192impl<'tcx> rustc_type_ir::inherent::BoundExistentialPredicates<TyCtxt<'tcx>>\n   270\u2192    for &'tcx ty::List<ty::PolyExistentialPredicate<'tcx>>\n   271\u2192{\n   272\u2192    fn principal_def_id(self) -> Option<DefId> {\n   273\u2192        self.principal_def_id()\n   274\u2192    }\n   275\u2192\n   276\u2192    fn principal(self) -> Option<ty::PolyExistentialTraitRef<'tcx>> {\n   277\u2192        self.principal()\n   278\u2192    }\n   279\u2192\n   280\u2192    fn auto_traits(self) -> impl IntoIterator<Item = DefId> {\n   281\u2192        self.auto_traits()\n   282\u2192    }\n   283\u2192\n   284\u2192    fn projection_bounds(\n   285\u2192        self,\n   286\u2192    ) -> impl IntoIterator<Item = ty::Binder<'tcx, ExistentialProjection<'tcx>>> {\n   287\u2192        self.projection_bounds()\n   288\u2192    }\n   289\u2192}\n   290\u2192\n   291\u2192impl<'tcx> ty::List<ty::PolyExistentialPredicate<'tcx>> {\n   292\u2192    /// Returns the \"principal `DefId`\" of this set of existential predicates.\n   293\u2192    ///\n   294\u2192    /// A Rust trait object type consists (in addition to a lifetime bound)\n   295\u2192    /// of a set of trait bounds, which are separated into any number\n   296\u2192    /// of auto-trait bounds, and at most one non-auto-trait bound. The\n   297\u2192    /// non-auto-trait bound is called the \"principal\" of the trait\n   298\u2192    /// object.\n   299\u2192    ///\n   300\u2192    /// Only the principal can have methods or type parameters (because\n   301\u2192    /// auto traits can have neither of them). This is important, because\n   302\u2192    /// it means the auto traits can be treated as an unordered set (methods\n   303\u2192    /// would force an order for the vtable, while relating traits with\n   304\u2192    /// type parameters without knowing the order to relate them in is\n   305\u2192    /// a rather non-trivial task).\n   306\u2192    ///\n   307\u2192    /// For example, in the trait object `dyn std::fmt::Debug + Sync`, the\n   308\u2192    /// principal bound is `Some(std::fmt::Debug)`, while the auto-trait bounds\n   309\u2192    /// are the set `{Sync}`.\n   310\u2192    ///\n   311\u2192    /// It is also possible to have a \"trivial\" trait object that\n   312\u2192    /// consists only of auto traits, with no principal - for example,\n   313\u2192    /// `dyn Send + Sync`. In that case, the set of auto-trait bounds\n   314\u2192    /// is `{Send, Sync}`, while there is no principal. These trait objects\n   315\u2192    /// have a \"trivial\" vtable consisting of just the size, alignment,\n   316\u2192    /// and destructor.\n   317\u2192    pub fn principal(&self) -> Option<ty::Binder<'tcx, ExistentialTraitRef<'tcx>>> {\n   318\u2192        self[0]\n   319\u2192            .map_bound(|this| match this {\n   320\u2192                ExistentialPredicate::Trait(tr) => Some(tr),\n   321\u2192                _ => None,\n   322\u2192            })\n   323\u2192            .transpose()\n   324\u2192    }\n   325\u2192\n   326\u2192    pub fn principal_def_id(&self) -> Option<DefId> {\n   327\u2192        self.principal().map(|trait_ref| trait_ref.skip_binder().def_id)\n   328\u2192    }\n   329\u2192\n   330\u2192    #[inline]\n   331\u2192    pub fn projection_bounds(\n   332\u2192        &self,\n   333\u2192    ) -> impl Iterator<Item = ty::Binder<'tcx, ExistentialProjection<'tcx>>> {\n   334\u2192        self.iter().filter_map(|predicate| {\n   335\u2192            predicate\n   336\u2192                .map_bound(|pred| match pred {\n   337\u2192                    ExistentialPredicate::Projection(projection) => Some(projection),\n   338\u2192                    _ => None,\n   339\u2192                })\n   340\u2192                .transpose()\n   341\u2192        })\n   342\u2192    }\n   343\u2192\n   344\u2192    #[inline]\n   345\u2192    pub fn auto_traits(&self) -> impl Iterator<Item = DefId> {\n   346\u2192        self.iter().filter_map(|predicate| match predicate.skip_binder() {\n   347\u2192            ExistentialPredicate::AutoTrait(did) => Some(did),\n   348\u2192            _ => None,\n   349\u2192        })\n   350\u2192    }\n   351\u2192\n   352\u2192    pub fn without_auto_traits(&self) -> impl Iterator<Item = ty::PolyExistentialPredicate<'tcx>> {\n   353\u2192        self.iter().filter(|predicate| {\n   354\u2192            !matches!(predicate.as_ref().skip_binder(), ExistentialPredicate::AutoTrait(_))\n   355\u2192        })\n   356\u2192    }\n   357\u2192}\n   358\u2192\n   359\u2192pub type PolyTraitRef<'tcx> = ty::Binder<'tcx, TraitRef<'tcx>>;\n   360\u2192pub type PolyExistentialTraitRef<'tcx> = ty::Binder<'tcx, ExistentialTraitRef<'tcx>>;\n   361\u2192pub type PolyExistentialProjection<'tcx> = ty::Binder<'tcx, ExistentialProjection<'tcx>>;\n   362\u2192\n   363\u2192impl<'tcx> Clause<'tcx> {\n   364\u2192    /// Performs a instantiation suitable for going from a\n   365\u2192    /// poly-trait-ref to supertraits that must hold if that\n   366\u2192    /// poly-trait-ref holds. This is slightly different from a normal\n   367\u2192    /// instantiation in terms of what happens with bound regions. See\n   368\u2192    /// lengthy comment below for details.\n   369\u2192    pub fn instantiate_supertrait(\n   370\u2192        self,\n   371\u2192        tcx: TyCtxt<'tcx>,\n   372\u2192        trait_ref: ty::PolyTraitRef<'tcx>,\n   373\u2192    ) -> Clause<'tcx> {\n   374\u2192        // The interaction between HRTB and supertraits is not entirely\n   375\u2192        // obvious. Let me walk you (and myself) through an example.\n   376\u2192        //\n   377\u2192        // Let's start with an easy case. Consider two traits:\n   378\u2192        //\n   379\u2192        //     trait Foo<'a>: Bar<'a,'a> { }\n   380\u2192        //     trait Bar<'b,'c> { }\n   381\u2192        //\n   382\u2192        // Now, if we have a trait reference `for<'x> T: Foo<'x>`, then\n   383\u2192        // we can deduce that `for<'x> T: Bar<'x,'x>`. Basically, if we\n   384\u2192        // knew that `Foo<'x>` (for any 'x) then we also know that\n   385\u2192        // `Bar<'x,'x>` (for any 'x). This more-or-less falls out from\n   386\u2192        // normal instantiation.\n   387\u2192        //\n   388\u2192        // In terms of why this is sound, the idea is that whenever there\n   389\u2192        // is an impl of `T:Foo<'a>`, it must show that `T:Bar<'a,'a>`\n   390\u2192        // holds. So if there is an impl of `T:Foo<'a>` that applies to\n   391\u2192        // all `'a`, then we must know that `T:Bar<'a,'a>` holds for all\n   392\u2192        // `'a`.\n   393\u2192        //\n   394\u2192        // Another example to be careful of is this:\n   395\u2192        //\n   396\u2192        //     trait Foo1<'a>: for<'b> Bar1<'a,'b> { }\n   397\u2192        //     trait Bar1<'b,'c> { }\n   398\u2192        //\n   399\u2192        // Here, if we have `for<'x> T: Foo1<'x>`, then what do we know?\n   400\u2192        // The answer is that we know `for<'x,'b> T: Bar1<'x,'b>`. The\n   401\u2192        // reason is similar to the previous example: any impl of\n   402\u2192        // `T:Foo1<'x>` must show that `for<'b> T: Bar1<'x, 'b>`. So\n   403\u2192        // basically we would want to collapse the bound lifetimes from\n   404\u2192        // the input (`trait_ref`) and the supertraits.\n   405\u2192        //\n   406\u2192        // To achieve this in practice is fairly straightforward. Let's\n   407\u2192        // consider the more complicated scenario:\n   408\u2192        //\n   409\u2192        // - We start out with `for<'x> T: Foo1<'x>`. In this case, `'x`\n   410\u2192        //   has a De Bruijn index of 1. We want to produce `for<'x,'b> T: Bar1<'x,'b>`,\n   411\u2192        //   where both `'x` and `'b` would have a DB index of 1.\n   412\u2192        //   The instantiation from the input trait-ref is therefore going to be\n   413\u2192        //   `'a => 'x` (where `'x` has a DB index of 1).\n   414\u2192        // - The supertrait-ref is `for<'b> Bar1<'a,'b>`, where `'a` is an\n   415\u2192        //   early-bound parameter and `'b` is a late-bound parameter with a\n   416\u2192        //   DB index of 1.\n   417\u2192        // - If we replace `'a` with `'x` from the input, it too will have\n   418\u2192        //   a DB index of 1, and thus we'll have `for<'x,'b> Bar1<'x,'b>`\n   419\u2192        //   just as we wanted.\n   420\u2192        //\n   421\u2192        // There is only one catch. If we just apply the instantiation `'a\n   422\u2192        // => 'x` to `for<'b> Bar1<'a,'b>`, the instantiation code will\n   423\u2192        // adjust the DB index because we instantiating into a binder (it\n   424\u2192        // tries to be so smart...) resulting in `for<'x> for<'b>\n   425\u2192        // Bar1<'x,'b>` (we have no syntax for this, so use your\n   426\u2192        // imagination). Basically the 'x will have DB index of 2 and 'b\n   427\u2192        // will have DB index of 1. Not quite what we want. So we apply\n   428\u2192        // the instantiation to the *contents* of the trait reference,\n   429\u2192        // rather than the trait reference itself (put another way, the\n   430\u2192        // instantiation code expects equal binding levels in the values\n   431\u2192        // from the instantiation and the value being instantiated into, and\n   432\u2192        // this trick achieves that).\n   433\u2192\n   434\u2192        // Working through the second example:\n   435\u2192        // trait_ref: for<'x> T: Foo1<'^0.0>; args: [T, '^0.0]\n   436\u2192        // predicate: for<'b> Self: Bar1<'a, '^0.0>; args: [Self, 'a, '^0.0]\n   437\u2192        // We want to end up with:\n   438\u2192        //     for<'x, 'b> T: Bar1<'^0.0, '^0.1>\n   439\u2192        // To do this:\n   440\u2192        // 1) We must shift all bound vars in predicate by the length\n   441\u2192        //    of trait ref's bound vars. So, we would end up with predicate like\n   442\u2192        //    Self: Bar1<'a, '^0.1>\n   443\u2192        // 2) We can then apply the trait args to this, ending up with\n   444\u2192        //    T: Bar1<'^0.0, '^0.1>\n   445\u2192        // 3) Finally, to create the final bound vars, we concatenate the bound\n   446\u2192        //    vars of the trait ref with those of the predicate:\n   447\u2192        //    ['x, 'b]\n   448\u2192        let bound_pred = self.kind();\n   449\u2192        let pred_bound_vars = bound_pred.bound_vars();\n   450\u2192        let trait_bound_vars = trait_ref.bound_vars();\n   451\u2192        // 1) Self: Bar1<'a, '^0.0> -> Self: Bar1<'a, '^0.1>\n   452\u2192        let shifted_pred =\n   453\u2192            tcx.shift_bound_var_indices(trait_bound_vars.len(), bound_pred.skip_binder());\n   454\u2192        // 2) Self: Bar1<'a, '^0.1> -> T: Bar1<'^0.0, '^0.1>\n   455\u2192        let new = EarlyBinder::bind(shifted_pred).instantiate(tcx, trait_ref.skip_binder().args);\n   456\u2192        // 3) ['x] + ['b] -> ['x, 'b]\n   457\u2192        let bound_vars =\n   458\u2192            tcx.mk_bound_variable_kinds_from_iter(trait_bound_vars.iter().chain(pred_bound_vars));\n   459\u2192\n   460\u2192        // FIXME: Is it really perf sensitive to use reuse_or_mk_predicate here?\n   461\u2192        tcx.reuse_or_mk_predicate(\n   462\u2192            self.as_predicate(),\n   463\u2192            ty::Binder::bind_with_vars(PredicateKind::Clause(new), bound_vars),\n   464\u2192        )\n   465\u2192        .expect_clause()\n   466\u2192    }\n   467\u2192}\n   468\u2192\n   469\u2192impl<'tcx> UpcastFrom<TyCtxt<'tcx>, PredicateKind<'tcx>> for Predicate<'tcx> {\n   470\u2192    fn upcast_from(from: PredicateKind<'tcx>, tcx: TyCtxt<'tcx>) -> Self {\n   471\u2192        ty::Binder::dummy(from).upcast(tcx)\n   472\u2192    }\n   473\u2192}\n   474\u2192\n   475\u2192impl<'tcx> UpcastFrom<TyCtxt<'tcx>, ty::Binder<'tcx, PredicateKind<'tcx>>> for Predicate<'tcx> {\n   476\u2192    fn upcast_from(from: ty::Binder<'tcx, PredicateKind<'tcx>>, tcx: TyCtxt<'tcx>) -> Self {\n    }\n}\n\nimpl<'tcx> ty::List<ty::PolyExistentialPredicate<'tcx>> {\n    /// Returns the \"principal `DefId`\" of this set of existential predicates.\n    ///\n    /// A Rust trait object type consists (in addition to a lifetime bound)\n    /// of a set of trait bounds, which are separated into any number\n    /// of auto-trait bounds, and at most one non-auto-trait bound. The\n    /// non-auto-trait bound is called the \"principal\" of the trait\n    /// object.\n    ///\n    /// Only the principal can have methods or type parameters (because\n    /// auto traits can have neither of them). This is important, because\n    /// it means the auto traits can be treated as an unordered set (methods\n    /// would force an order for the vtable, while relating traits with\n    /// type parameters without knowing the order to relate them in is\n    /// a rather non-trivial task).\n    ///\n    /// For example, in the trait object `dyn std::fmt::Debug + Sync`, the\n    /// principal bound is `Some(std::fmt::Debug)`, while the auto-trait bounds\n    /// are the set `{Sync}`.\n    ///\n    /// It is also possible to have a \"trivial\" trait object that\n    /// consists only of auto traits, with no principal - for example,\n    /// `dyn Send + Sync`. In that case, the set of auto-trait bounds\n    /// is `{Send, Sync}`, while there is no principal. These trait objects\n    /// have a \"trivial\" vtable consisting of just the size, alignment,\n    /// and destructor.\n    pub fn principal(&self) -> Option<ty::Binder<'tcx, ExistentialTraitRef<'tcx>>> {\n        self[0]\n            .map_bound(|this| match this {\n                ExistentialPredicate::Trait(tr) => Some(tr),\n                _ => None,\n            })\n            .transpose()\n    }\n\n    pub fn principal_def_id(&self) -> Option<DefId> {\n        self.principal().map(|trait_ref| trait_ref.skip_binder().def_id)\n    }\n\n    #[inline]\n    pub fn projection_bounds(\n        &self,\n    ) -> impl Iterator<Item = ty::Binder<'tcx, ExistentialProjection<'tcx>>> {\n        self.iter().filter_map(|predicate| {\n            predicate\n                .map_bound(|pred| match pred {\n                    ExistentialPredicate::Projection(projection) => Some(projection),\n                    _ => None,\n                })\n                .transpose()\n        })\n    }\n\n    #[inline]\n    pub fn auto_traits(&self) -> impl Iterator<Item = DefId> {\n        self.iter().filter_map(|predicate| match predicate.skip_binder() {\n            ExistentialPredicate::AutoTrait(did) => Some(did),\n            _ => None,\n        })\n    }\n\n    pub fn without_auto_traits(&self) -> impl Iterator<Item = ty::PolyExistentialPredicate<'tcx>> {\n        self.iter().filter(|predicate| {\n            !matches!(predicate.as_ref().skip_binder(), ExistentialPredicate::AutoTrait(_))\n        })\n    }\n}\n\npub type PolyTraitRef<'tcx> = ty::Binder<'tcx, TraitRef<'tcx>>;\npub type PolyExistentialTraitRef<'tcx> = ty::Binder<'tcx, ExistentialTraitRef<'tcx>>;\npub type PolyExistentialProjection<'tcx> = ty::Binder<'tcx, ExistentialProjection<'tcx>>;\n\nimpl<'tcx> Clause<'tcx> {\n    /// Performs a instantiation suitable for going from a\n    /// poly-trait-ref to supertraits that must hold if that\n    /// poly-trait-ref holds. This is slightly different from a normal\n    /// instantiation in terms of what happens with bound regions. See\n    /// lengthy comment below for details.\n    pub fn instantiate_supertrait(\n        self,\n        tcx: TyCtxt<'tcx>,\n        trait_ref: ty::PolyTraitRef<'tcx>,\n    ) -> Clause<'tcx> {\n        // The interaction between HRTB and supertraits is not entirely\n        // obvious. Let me walk you (and myself) through an example.\n        //\n        // Let's start with an easy case. Consider two traits:\n        //\n        //     trait Foo<'a>: Bar<'a,'a> { }\n        //     trait Bar<'b,'c> { }\n        //\n        // Now, if we have a trait reference `for<'x> T: Foo<'x>`, then\n        // we can deduce that `for<'x> T: Bar<'x,'x>`. Basically, if we\n        // knew that `Foo<'x>` (for any 'x) then we also know that\n        // `Bar<'x,'x>` (for any 'x). This more-or-less falls out from\n        // normal instantiation.\n        //\n        // In terms of why this is sound, the idea is that whenever there\n        // is an impl of `T:Foo<'a>`, it must show that `T:Bar<'a,'a>`\n        // holds. So if there is an impl of `T:Foo<'a>` that applies to\n        // all `'a`, then we must know that `T:Bar<'a,'a>` holds for all\n        // `'a`.\n        //\n        // Another example to be careful of is this:\n        //\n        //     trait Foo1<'a>: for<'b> Bar1<'a,'b> { }\n        //     trait Bar1<'b,'c> { }\n        //\n        // Here, if we have `for<'x> T: Foo1<'x>`, then what do we know?\n        // The answer is that we know `for<'x,'b> T: Bar1<'x,'b>`. The\n        // reason is similar to the previous example: any impl of\n        // `T:Foo1<'x>` must show that `for<'b> T: Bar1<'x, 'b>`. So\n        // basically we would want to collapse the bound lifetimes from\n        // the input (`trait_ref`) and the supertraits.\n        //\n        // To achieve this in practice is fairly straightforward. Let's\n        // consider the more complicated scenario:\n        //\n        // - We start out with `for<'x> T: Foo1<'x>`. In this case, `'x`\n        //   has a De Bruijn index of 1. We want to produce `for<'x,'b> T: Bar1<'x,'b>`,\n        //   where both `'x` and `'b` would have a DB index of 1.\n        //   The instantiation from the input trait-ref is therefore going to be\n        //   `'a => 'x` (where `'x` has a DB index of 1).\n        // - The supertrait-ref is `for<'b> Bar1<'a,'b>`, where `'a` is an\n        //   early-bound parameter and `'b` is a late-bound parameter with a\n        //   DB index of 1.\n        // - If we replace `'a` with `'x` from the input, it too will have\n        //   a DB index of 1, and thus we'll have `for<'x,'b> Bar1<'x,'b>`\n        //   just as we wanted.\n        //\n        // There is only one catch. If we just apply the instantiation `'a\n        // => 'x` to `for<'b> Bar1<'a,'b>`, the instantiation code will\n        // adjust the DB index because we instantiating into a binder (it\n        // tries to be so smart...) resulting in `for<'x> for<'b>\n        // Bar1<'x,'b>` (we have no syntax for this, so use your\n        // imagination). Basically the 'x will have DB index of 2 and 'b\n        // will have DB index of 1. Not quite what we want. So we apply\n        // the instantiation to the *contents* of the trait reference,\n        // rather than the trait reference itself (put another way, the\n        // instantiation code expects equal binding levels in the values\n        // from the instantiation and the value being instantiated into, and\n        // this trick achieves that).\n\n        // Working through the second example:\n        // trait_ref: for<'x> T: Foo1<'^0.0>; args: [T, '^0.0]\n        // predicate: for<'b> Self: Bar1<'a, '^0.0>; args: [Self, 'a, '^0.0]\n        // We want to end up with:\n        //     for<'x, 'b> T: Bar1<'^0.0, '^0.1>\n        // To do this:\n        // 1) We must shift all bound vars in predicate by the length\n        //    of trait ref's bound vars. So, we would end up with predicate like\n        //    Self: Bar1<'a, '^0.1>\n        // 2) We can then apply the trait args to this, ending up with\n        //    T: Bar1<'^0.0, '^0.1>\n        // 3) Finally, to create the final bound vars, we concatenate the bound\n        //    vars of the trait ref with those of the predicate:\n        //    ['x, 'b]\n        let bound_pred = self.kind();\n        let pred_bound_vars = bound_pred.bound_vars();\n        let trait_bound_vars = trait_ref.bound_vars();\n        // 1) Self: Bar1<'a, '^0.0> -> Self: Bar1<'a, '^0.1>\n        let shifted_pred =\n            tcx.shift_bound_var_indices(trait_bound_vars.len(), bound_pred.skip_binder());\n        // 2) Self: Bar1<'a, '^0.1> -> T: Bar1<'^0.0, '^0.1>\n        let new = EarlyBinder::bind(shifted_pred).instantiate(tcx, trait_ref.skip_binder().args);\n        // 3) ['x] + ['b] -> ['x, 'b]\n        let bound_vars =\n            tcx.mk_bound_variable_kinds_from_iter(trait_bound_vars.iter().chain(pred_bound_vars));\n\n        // FIXME: Is it really perf sensitive to use reuse_or_mk_predicate here?\n        tcx.reuse_or_mk_predicate(\n            self.as_predicate(),\n            ty::Binder::bind_with_vars(PredicateKind::Clause(new), bound_vars),\n        )\n        .expect_clause()\n    }\n}\n\nimpl<'tcx> UpcastFrom<TyCtxt<'tcx>, PredicateKind<'tcx>> for Predicate<'tcx> {\n    fn upcast_from(from: PredicateKind<'tcx>, tcx: TyCtxt<'tcx>) -> Self {\n        ty::Binder::dummy(from).upcast(tcx)\n    }\n}\n\nimpl<'tcx> UpcastFrom<TyCtxt<'tcx>, ty::Binder<'tcx, PredicateKind<'tcx>>> for Predicate<'tcx> {\n    fn upcast_from(from: ty::Binder<'tcx, PredicateKind<'tcx>>, tcx: TyCtxt<'tcx>) -> Self {\n        tcx.mk_predicate(from)\n    }\n}\n\nimpl<'tcx> UpcastFrom<TyCtxt<'tcx>, ClauseKind<'tcx>> for Predicate<'tcx> {\n    fn upcast_from(from: ClauseKind<'tcx>, tcx: TyCtxt<'tcx>) -> Self {\n        tcx.mk_predicate(ty::Binder::dummy(PredicateKind::Clause(from)))\n    }\n}\n\nimpl<'tcx> UpcastFrom<TyCtxt<'tcx>, ty::Binder<'tcx, ClauseKind<'tcx>>> for Predicate<'tcx> {\n    fn upcast_from(from: ty::Binder<'tcx, ClauseKind<'tcx>>, tcx: TyCtxt<'tcx>) -> Self {\n        tcx.mk_predicate(from.map_bound(PredicateKind::Clause))\n    }\n}\n\nimpl<'tcx> UpcastFrom<TyCtxt<'tcx>, Clause<'tcx>> for Predicate<'tcx> {\n    fn upcast_from(from: Clause<'tcx>, _tcx: TyCtxt<'tcx>) -> Self {\n        from.as_predicate()\n    }\n}\n\nimpl<'tcx> UpcastFrom<TyCtxt<'tcx>, ClauseKind<'tcx>> for Clause<'tcx> {\n    fn upcast_from(from: ClauseKind<'tcx>, tcx: TyCtxt<'tcx>) -> Self {\n        tcx.mk_predicate(ty::Binder::dummy(PredicateKind::Clause(from))).expect_clause()\n    }\n}\n\nimpl<'tcx> UpcastFrom<TyCtxt<'tcx>, ty::Binder<'tcx, ClauseKind<'tcx>>> for Clause<'tcx> {\n    fn }\n}\n\nimpl<'tcx> UpcastFrom<TyCtxt<'tcx>, TraitRef<'tcx>> for Predicate<'tcx> {\n    fn upcast_from(from: TraitRef<'tcx>, tcx: TyCtxt<'tcx>) -> Self {\n        ty::Binder::dummy(from).upcast(tcx)\n /// Encodes that `a` must be a subtype of `b`. The `a_is_expected` flag indicates
/// whether the `a` type is the type that we should label as "expected" when
/// presenting user diagnostics.
#[derive_where(Clone, Copy, Hash, PartialEq, Debug; I: Interner)]
#[derive(TypeVisitable_Generic, TypeFoldable_Generic, Lift_Generic)]
#[cfg_attr(
    feature = "nightly",
    derive(Decodable_NoContext, Encodable_NoContext, HashStable_NoContext)
)]
pub struct SubtypePredicate<I: Interner> {
    pub a_is_expected: bool,
    pub a: I::Ty,
    pub b: I::Ty,
}

impl<I: Interner> Eq for SubtypePredicate<I> {}
After
/// Encodes that `sub_ty` must be a subtype of `super_ty`. The `a_is_expected` flag indicates
/// whether the `sub_ty` type is the type that we should label as "expected" when
/// presenting user diagnostics.
#[derive_where(Clone, Copy, Hash, PartialEq, Debug; I: Interner)]
#[derive(TypeVisitable_Generic, TypeFoldable_Generic, Lift_Generic)]
#[cfg_attr(
    feature = "nightly",
    derive(Decodable_NoContext, Encodable_NoContext, HashStable_NoContext)
)]
pub struct SubtypeRelation<I: Interner> {
    pub a_is_expected: bool,
    pub sub_ty: I::Ty,
    pub super_ty: I::Ty,
}

impl<I: Interner> Eq for SubtypeRelation<I> {}

# Rust Compiler Refactoring: SubtypePredicate → SubtypeRelation

## Summary
This document provides a complete analysis of the refactoring needed to rename `SubtypePredicate` to `SubtypeRelation` and its fields `a`/`b` to `sub_ty`/`super_ty` throughout the Rust compiler. The refactoring improves semantic clarity by making field names explicit about their role in the subtype relation.

## Files Examined

### Core Definition Files
- **compiler/rustc_type_ir/src/predicate.rs** — Struct definition (lines 918-924): Contains the original `SubtypePredicate<I>` struct with fields `a_is_expected`, `a`, and `b`. **Needs change**: Rename struct and fields.
- **compiler/rustc_type_ir/src/predicate_kind.rs** — PredicateKind enum (line 78): `Subtype(ty::SubtypePredicate<I>)` variant definition. **Needs change**: Update type annotation to `SubtypeRelation`.

### Type Aliases (rustc_middle)
- **compiler/rustc_middle/src/ty/predicate.rs** (lines 24, 32): Type aliases that re-export from rustc_type_ir:
  - `pub type SubtypePredicate<'tcx> = ir::SubtypePredicate<TyCtxt<'tcx>>;`
  - `pub type PolySubtypePredicate<'tcx> = ty::Binder<'tcx, SubtypePredicate<'tcx>>;`
  - **Needs change**: Both need to be updated to use `SubtypeRelation`.

- **compiler/rustc_middle/src/ty/mod.rs** — Re-exports via pub use. **Needs change**: Update re-export statements.

### Struct Literal Construction Sites (Creating new instances)
- **compiler/rustc_type_ir/src/relate/solver_relating.rs**:
  - Lines 200-204: `ty::PredicateKind::Subtype(ty::SubtypePredicate { a_is_expected: true, a, b })`
  - Lines 213-217: `ty::PredicateKind::Subtype(ty::SubtypePredicate { a_is_expected: false, a: b, b: a })` (swapped)
  - **Needs change**: Update field names to `sub_ty` and `super_ty`.

- **compiler/rustc_infer/src/infer/relat
... (truncated)

## Changes by Category

### Struct Definition Changes
```
OLD: pub struct SubtypePredicate<I: Interner> { pub a: I::Ty, pub b: I::Ty, ... }
NEW: pub struct SubtypeRelation<I: Interner> { pub sub_ty: I::Ty, pub super_ty: I::Ty, ... }
```

### Type Annotation Changes
```
OLD: Subtype(ty::SubtypePredicate<I>)
NEW: Subtype(ty::SubtypeRelation<I>)
```

### Struct Literal Changes (4 instances)

NEW: ty::SubtypeRelation { a_is_expected: true, sub_ty: x, super_ty: y }
```

### Pattern Match Changes (6 instances)
```
OLD: ty::PredicateKind::Subtype(ty::SubtypePredicate { a_is_expected: _, a, b })
NEW: ty::PredicateKind::Subtype(ty::SubtypeRelation { a_is_expected: _, sub_ty, super_ty })
```

## Semantic Preservation

The refactoring preserves all semantics:

1. **Type Relations**: The meaning of "sub_ty <: super_ty" remains unchanged
2. **Field Order**: Fields maintain their original logical order
3. **Expected Flag**: The `a_is_expected` field maintains its original purpose
4. **Swapped Cases**: Where fields are intentionally swapped for bidirectional relations, the semantic meaning is preserved

### Example: Bidirectional Relations
```
// OLD: Semantically unclear why 'a' and 'b' are swapped
ty::SubtypePredicate { a_is_expected: false, a: b, b: a }

// NEW: Clear that when a_is_expected is false, we're checking super_ty <: sub_ty
ty::SubtypeRelation { a_is_expected: false, sub_ty: b, super_ty: a }
```

---

## Backward Compatibility

For external consumers of `rustc_public`, optional deprecation aliases can be maintained:

```rust
// In rustc_middle/ty/predicate.rs
pub type SubtypePredicate<'tcx> = SubtypeRelation<'tcx>;  // Deprecated
pub type PolySubtypePredicate<'tcx> = PolySubtypeRelation<'tcx>;  // Deprecated

// In rustc_public/src/ty.rs
#[deprecated = "Use SubtypeRelation instead"]
pub type SubtypePredicate = SubtypeRelation;
```

This allows gradual migration for downstream tools.
